## 28. Weak password policy (1-character passwords accepted) combined with unsalted MD5 password hashing and password_hash/totp_secret exposure in API responses

- Severity: medium
- OWASP: A07
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/register
- CVSS: 5.9 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:H/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The registration endpoint at /api/auth/register accepts passwords as short as a single character with no minimum length or complexity enforcement. Passwords are hashed using unsalted MD5, a fast, reversible-by-rainbow-table algorithm. Additionally, the register/login/profile API responses include the raw password_hash and totp_secret fields directly in the JSON payload returned to the client.

### Impact
Weak, unsalted MD5 hashes of 1-character passwords can be cracked in milliseconds via brute force or precomputed rainbow tables. Because password_hash (and totp_secret) are returned in API responses, any actor able to trigger registration, login, or profile endpoints — including an unauthenticated self-registration flow — can harvest crackable password hashes and TOTP secrets for accounts, enabling credential recovery and potential account takeover or 2FA bypass.

### Likelihood
Certain - reproduced directly by registering an account with a 1-character password and observing the unsalted MD5 hash returned in the API response.

### Recommendation
Enforce a minimum password length (>=8 characters) and complexity rules at registration and password-change endpoints. Migrate password storage to bcrypt or argon2 with a unique per-user salt, and apply this uniformly across all accounts (including any legacy users still on MD5). Remove password_hash and totp_secret from all client-facing API responses; these values should never leave the server.

### Evidence
```
POST /api/auth/register with password="a" (1 character) succeeded with HTTP 201, returning {"success":true,"data":{"user":{"id":48,"email":"aespa_bb688f53@example.invalid",...,"password_hash":"694b9d51df47149ccd1aa8e3c0e10e60",...},"token":"[REDACTED_JWT]"},"message":"Registration successful"}. The response leaks a raw, unsalted 32-hex-char MD5 digest of the 1-character password directly to the client.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST /api/auth/register {"email":"aespa_bb688f53@example.invalid","password":"a","first_name":"Test","last_name":"Weakpw"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
HTTP 201 {"success":true,"data":{"user":{"id":48,...,"password_hash":"694b9d51df47149ccd1aa8e3c0e10e60",...}}}
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 29. Missing rate-limiting / account lockout on login endpoint

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A07
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/login
- CVSS: 3.7 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:N/I:N/A:L)

### Description
The login endpoint at POST /api/auth/login does not implement any rate-limiting, progressive delay, CAPTCHA, or account/IP lockout mechanism after repeated failed authentication attempts. This was verified by submitting six consecutive login requests for a known valid email address (amelia.chen@example.com) with different incorrect passwords.

### Impact
Without throttling, an attacker can perform unlimited automated password-guessing attempts (brute force or credential stuffing) against any known or enumerated email address. This risk is compounded when combined with other weaknesses such as user enumeration or weak password hashing, as it increases the practical feasibility of successfully guessing valid credentials over time.

### Likelihood
Certain - directly reproduced. All 6 consecutive attempts returned identical HTTP 401 responses with no CAPTCHA, no increasing delay, no HTTP 429, and no lockout indication.

### Recommendation
Implement rate-limiting (e.g., per-IP and per-account) with progressive delays, CAPTCHA challenges, or temporary account/IP lockout after a small number of failed login attempts (e.g., 5). Return HTTP 429 with appropriate Retry-After headers once thresholds are exceeded. Apply equivalent protections to the forgot-password and TOTP verification endpoints to prevent similar abuse.

### Evidence
```
Sent 6 consecutive POST /api/auth/login requests for amelia.chen@example.com with wrong passwords (wrongpass1..wrongpass6). All 6 attempts returned HTTP 401 with an identical WRONG_PASSWORD-style error; no CAPTCHA, no increasing delay, no HTTP 429, and no lockout message observed on the 6th attempt.
```

### Request Evidence
```
6x POST /api/auth/login {"email":"amelia.chen@example.com","password":"wrongpassN"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
All 6 attempts: HTTP 401, no throttling/lockout/captcha observed
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 30. Missing security headers (X-Frame-Options, X-Content-Type-Options, CSP) on customer banking SPA

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/banking/
- CVSS: 3.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:R/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The customer-facing banking single-page application served at /banking/ (and /banking/index.html) does not return standard security response headers. Responses include only server, content-length, and content-type, with no X-Frame-Options, X-Content-Type-Options, Content-Security-Policy, Strict-Transport-Security, or Referrer-Policy headers. In the same environment, /admin/ and /api/* consistently return x-frame-options: DENY, x-content-type-options: nosniff, x-xss-protection: 1; mode=block, and referrer-policy: strict-origin-when-cross-origin, showing the banking SPA is inconsistently hardened compared to other application surfaces.

### Impact
Absence of X-Frame-Options/CSP frame-ancestors on the banking SPA permits the page to be embedded in an attacker-controlled iframe, enabling UI-redressing/clickjacking attacks against authenticated banking functionality (e.g., transfers). Lack of X-Content-Type-Options allows browsers to MIME-sniff responses, increasing risk if any user-influenced or injected content is ever served from this path. No sensitive data exposure or authentication bypass was demonstrated; impact is limited to increased susceptibility to client-side attacks that would still require separate conditions (e.g., user interaction or an injection vector) to be exploitable.

### Likelihood
High - confirmed missing on every observed request to /banking/ and /banking/index.html, while the same headers are consistently present on /admin/ and /api/* in the identical environment, indicating an inconsistent header policy rather than a one-off gap.

### Recommendation
Apply the same security headers already used on /admin/ and /api/* to all responses served from /banking/: X-Frame-Options: DENY (or equivalent frame-ancestors 'none'/'self' in CSP), X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff, a Content-Security-Policy appropriate for the SPA, Referrer-Policy: strict-origin-when-cross-origin, and Strict-Transport-Security if served over HTTPS. Centralize header configuration (e.g., at the reverse proxy or framework middleware level) so all application paths inherit a consistent baseline policy.

### Evidence
```
GET /banking/ response headers included only server: Apache/2.4.58 (Ubuntu), content-length: 6294, content-type: text/html, with no X-Frame-Options, X-Content-Type-Options, Content-Security-Policy, Strict-Transport-Security, or Referrer-Policy headers. By contrast, /admin/ and /api/* responses in the same environment consistently include x-content-type-options: nosniff, x-frame-options: DENY, x-xss-protection: 1; mode=block, and referrer-policy: strict-origin-when-cross-origin.
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET http://100.96.0.19/banking/
```

### Response Evidence
```
Headers observed: server, content-length, content-type only — missing x-frame-options, x-content-type-options, content-security-policy (present on /admin/ responses in the same environment)
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 31. Overly permissive CORS — arbitrary Origin reflection with credentials, enabling cross-origin attacks from any domain

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Middleware/CorsMiddleware.php:7-17
- CVSS: 3.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:R/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The CORS middleware reflects whatever `Origin` header the client supplied, sets `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true`, and permits every header. A malicious site can issue authenticated cross-origin requests to the API on behalf of a logged-in victim. Combined with the JWT sig-bypass and the SSRF, the cross-origin attack surface is wide.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed via Origin: https://evil.example header reflected verbatim in Access-Control-Allow-Origin response header on /api/health.

### Evidence
```
public static function handle(): void\n{\n    $origin = $_SERVER['HTTP_ORIGIN'] ?? '*';\n\n    header(\"Access-Control-Allow-Origin: {$origin}\");\n    header('Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true');\n    header('Access-Control-Allow-Methods: GET, POST, PUT, DELETE, OPTIONS, PATCH, HEAD');\n    header('Access-Control-Allow-Headers: *');\n    header('Access-Control-Max-Age: 86400');\n    header('Content-Type: application/json; charset=utf-8');\n\n    if ($_SERVER['REQUEST_METHOD'] === 'OPTIONS') {\n        http_response_code(200);\n        exit;\n    }\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed via Origin: https://evil.example header reflected verbatim in Access-Control-Allow-Origin response header on /api/health.

## 32. Overly Permissive CORS Policy — Arbitrary Origin Reflected with Credentials Enabled

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/health
- CVSS: 3.1 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:R/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The application's CORS middleware (CorsMiddleware.php) reflects any client-supplied Origin header back in the Access-Control-Allow-Origin response header rather than validating it against an allow-list. This was confirmed on the /api/health endpoint, where sending Origin: https://evil.example caused the server to respond with Access-Control-Allow-Origin: https://evil.example. Source code review further confirms that Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true is set globally alongside this reflected origin behavior.

### Impact
Because the reflected origin is combined with Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true, a malicious website could potentially issue cross-origin XHR/fetch requests with the victim's credentials and read responses from sensitive endpoints (e.g. /api/profile, /api/accounts, /api/transactions) if those endpoints exhibit the same CORS behavior. This finding demonstrates the underlying misconfiguration via a header-reflection test on /api/health; no browser-based proof-of-concept demonstrating actual theft of authenticated, sensitive data was captured.

### Likelihood
High — the arbitrary-origin reflection combined with credentials support was directly confirmed via header inspection and source code review, though exploitation impact depends on which authenticated endpoints share this same middleware configuration.

### Recommendation
Replace origin reflection in CorsMiddleware.php with a strict server-side allow-list of trusted origins. Never combine a wildcard or reflected Origin value with Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true. Explicitly validate the Origin header against known trusted domains before echoing it back, and ensure preflight (OPTIONS) responses enforce the same policy.

### Evidence
```
Request with Origin: https://evil.example returned Access-Control-Allow-Origin: https://evil.example (reflecting arbitrary origin) on /api/health. Source code confirms Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true is also set globally (CorsMiddleware.php).
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET /api/health with header Origin: https://evil.example
```

### Response Evidence
```
access-control-allow-origin: https://evil.example
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 33. Stack trace and file path disclosure in 500 error responses

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: public/index.php:25-31
- CVSS: 3.7 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The global exception handler in `public/index.php` catches every uncaught Throwable and returns the full `$e->getMessage()`, `$e->getFile()`, `$e->getLine()`, and `$e->getTraceAsString()` to the client. This leaks absolute server paths, function names, library versions, and occasionally secrets stored in environment variables — all useful for further pivoting. Any handler that throws (or unhandled error path) is a disclosure event.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - triggered an uncaught TypeError via malformed JWT (missing jti), server returned full file path, line number, and stack trace in JSON response body with HTTP 500.

### Evidence
```
} catch (\\Throwable $e) {\n    Response::error('INTERNAL_ERROR', $e->getMessage(), 500, [\n        'file'  => $e->getFile(),\n        'line'  => $e->getLine(),\n        'trace' => $e->getTraceAsString(),\n    ]);\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - triggered an uncaught TypeError via malformed JWT (missing jti), server returned full file path, line number, and stack trace in JSON response body with HTTP 500.

## 34. User enumeration on customer login — distinct error codes for unknown email vs wrong password

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A07
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Controllers/AuthController.php:46-63
- CVSS: 3.7 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
POST /api/auth/login returns `USER_NOT_FOUND` when the email doesn't exist and `WRONG_PASSWORD` when the email exists but the password is wrong. This lets an attacker enumerate valid customer emails before launching a credential-stuffing or MD5-cracking attack. No rate-limiting either, making enumeration trivial.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
POST /api/auth/login with unknown email returned {"code":"USER_NOT_FOUND"}. Will compare against known valid email + wrong password next to confirm differential.

### Evidence
```
if (!$user) {\n    Response::error('USER_NOT_FOUND', 'No account found with this email address.', 401);\n}\n\nif (!AuthService::verifyPassword($data['password'], $user['password_hash'])) {\n    Response::error('WRONG_PASSWORD', 'Incorrect password.', 401);\n}
```

### Validation Note
POST /api/auth/login with unknown email returned {"code":"USER_NOT_FOUND"}. Will compare against known valid email + wrong password next to confirm differential.

## 35. User enumeration on customer login — distinct error codes for unknown email vs wrong password

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A07
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/auth/login
- CVSS: 3.7 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The login endpoint at /api/auth/login returns distinguishable error codes and messages depending on whether the submitted email exists in the system. Requests with an unregistered email return {"code":"USER_NOT_FOUND","message":"No account found with this email address."} (HTTP 401), while requests with a registered email and an incorrect password return {"code":"WRONG_PASSWORD","message":"Incorrect password."} (HTTP 401). This behavioral difference allows an attacker to determine whether a given email address is a registered customer account.

### Impact
An attacker can enumerate valid customer email addresses by observing the distinct error code returned by the login endpoint, which could support targeted credential stuffing, password spraying, or phishing campaigns against confirmed accounts.

### Likelihood
High - the differential is trivially observable via a single unauthenticated request and no rate limiting was observed on repeated login attempts, making automated enumeration straightforward.

### Recommendation
Return an identical generic error code and message (e.g. INVALID_CREDENTIALS) for both unknown-email and wrong-password cases, ensuring response timing is also consistent. Implement rate-limiting and/or account lockout/backoff on the login endpoint to slow automated enumeration attempts.

### Evidence
```
POST with unknown email returns {"code":"USER_NOT_FOUND","message":"No account found with this email address."} (401). POST with known email amelia.chen@example.com + wrong password returns {"code":"WRONG_PASSWORD","message":"Incorrect password."} (401). No rate limiting observed on repeated attempts.
```

### Request Evidence
```
POST /api/auth/login {"email":"nonexistent-user-zzz@example.com","password":"password"} vs {"email":"amelia.chen@example.com","password":"definitely_wrong_password_123"}
```

### Response Evidence
```
Unknown: {"success":false,"error":{"code":"USER_NOT_FOUND",...}} | Known: {"success":false,"error":{"code":"WRONG_PASSWORD",...}}
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 36. Verbose Error Handling Exposes Stack Traces and Absolute File Paths

- Severity: low
- OWASP: A05
- Source: Dynamic
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: http://100.96.0.19/api/profile
- CVSS: 3.7 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:H/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:N/A:N)

### Description
The /api/profile endpoint's global exception handler does not sanitize errors before returning them to the client. Submitting a malformed JWT (missing the jti claim) triggers an uncaught PHP TypeError in AuthService::isTokenRevoked(), which is surfaced directly in the HTTP 500 JSON response. The response includes the full exception message, absolute server file paths (e.g. /var/www/bankofed/src/Services/AuthService.php), class/method names, line numbers, and a partial call stack spanning AuthMiddleware.php, Router.php, and public/index.php.

### Impact
Discloses internal application structure, absolute filesystem paths, class/method names, and call-stack details to any client able to trigger an unhandled exception, including unauthenticated or malformed requests. This aids attacker reconnaissance (framework internals, source layout, technology stack) and could occasionally leak sensitive values if secrets happen to be embedded in future exception messages. No credentials, tokens, or other high-entropy secrets were exposed in the observed response.

### Likelihood
High - reliably reproducible by sending any malformed/incomplete JWT (e.g. missing jti claim) to a typed parameter in the authentication flow; no authentication or special access is required to trigger it.

### Recommendation
Wrap request handling in a global exception handler that catches all uncaught exceptions/errors and returns a generic, sanitized error message (e.g. {"code":"INTERNAL_ERROR","message":"An unexpected error occurred."}) with no file, line, or trace fields in the response body. Log full stack traces and exception details server-side only. Disable display_errors and any debug/detailed-error modes in production configuration, and add input validation/type checks in AuthMiddleware before calling AuthService::isTokenRevoked() so malformed tokens are rejected with a controlled 401 response rather than propagating a TypeError.

### Evidence
```
GET /api/profile with a malformed JWT (missing jti claim) triggered an uncaught TypeError, returned as HTTP 500 with full internal detail: message references BankOfEd\Services\AuthService::isTokenRevoked() argument type error; details.file=/var/www/bankofed/src/Services/AuthService.php, line=77; trace includes /var/www/bankofed/src/Middleware/AuthMiddleware.php(28), Router.php(101), and public/index.php(26).
```

### Request Evidence
```
GET /api/profile with Authorization: Bearer &lt;JWT missing jti claim&gt;
```

### Response Evidence
```
HTTP 500 {"success":false,"error":{"code":"INTERNAL_ERROR","message":"...Argument #1 ($jti) must be of type string, null given...","details":{"file":"/var/www/bankofed/src/Services/AuthService.php","line":77,"trace":"..."}}}
```

### Validation Note
Adversarial validator exhausted the step budget without finding a disproof.

## 37. BOPLA / mass-assignment IDOR in profile update — `user_id` body field lets any user modify another user's profile

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A01
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Controllers/ProfileController.php:21-58
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
PUT /api/profile accepts a `user_id` field in the JSON body and uses it (cast to int) as the target of `User::update()`. The authenticated user is supposed to be able to update only their own profile, but the handler honours whatever user_id is supplied. Any authenticated user can overwrite another user's email, phone, address, etc. Additionally, `User::update()`'s whitelist includes `avatar_url`, `totp_secret`, and `totp_enabled`, so a crafted body could potentially escalate further (depending on whether those fields are reachable via `$updateData`).

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation

### Evidence

## 38. IDOR / BFLA in external transfer — `from_account_id` not checked against authenticated user, enabling theft from any account

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A01
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Services/TransferService.php:172-178
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
POST /api/transfers/external resolves the source account via `Account::findById($fromAccountId)` instead of `Account::findByIdAndUser()`. Any authenticated user can supply any account ID in the bank as the source of an external transfer and drain funds from accounts they do not own — credits an attacker's account, or sends to an external payee. Combined with the absence of a balance check and TOTP bypass, an attacker can fully drain arbitrary victim accounts.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed directly - user 51 (zero owned accounts) executed POST /api/transfers/external with from_account_id=1 (owned by amelia.chen) and successfully debited $1.00 from that account, HTTP 201 with new_from_balance reflecting the debit.

### Evidence
```
// VULNERABILITY #23: IDOR - no ownership check on source account.\n// Any authenticated user can supply any account ID as the source.\n$fromAccount = Account::findById($fromAccountId);\nif (!$fromAccount) {\n    Response::notFound('Source account not found.');\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed directly - user 51 (zero owned accounts) executed POST /api/transfers/external with from_account_id=1 (owned by amelia.chen) and successfully debited $1.00 from that account, HTTP 201 with new_from_balance reflecting the debit.

GET /api/transactions/{id} fetches the transaction by ID without any ownership verification (no check on `user['id']` vs the accounts involved). Any authenticated user can iterate IDs 1..N and view every other customer's transaction details (BSB, account numbers, amounts, descriptions).

- Severity: info

- Source: sast lead

### Description
Combining finding #4 (user_id mass-assignment) with the email-uniqueness check at lines 36-42: an attacker overwrites any victim's email to an attacker-controlled address, then issues a password reset (or exploits the customer-side missing password reset flow with the new email). Effectively full account takeover of any user. The defence line (email uniqueness check) only checks uniqueness, not authorisation; it does NOT prevent targeting another user.

### Impact

### Likelihood


### Validation Note

## 41. JWT signature verification bypass — `decodeToken` only checks expiration, ignores HMAC signature entirely

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A08
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Services/AuthService.php:42-56
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
The `AuthService::decodeToken()` method splits the JWT into parts and base64-decodes the payload only. It never re-derives the HMAC with the configured `jwt_secret`, so any attacker can craft a token with arbitrary `sub` (user_id) and `exp` (future timestamp) and impersonate ANY user — including admins on the separate admin JWT path? No, this is the customer JWT. But also no `iss` issuer check. Token forging is total.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - forged a JWT with a completely bogus/wrong secret (not the leaked jwt_secret) for user_id=1 and it was accepted by /api/profile, returning user 1's full profile data with HTTP 200. Signature is not verified at all server-side.

### Evidence
```
public static function decodeToken(string $token): ?object\n{\n    try {\n        // Decode token payload without strict signature verification\n        $parts = explode('.', $token);\n        if (count($parts) !== 3) {\n            return null;\n        }\n        $payload = json_decode(base64_decode(strtr($parts[1], '-_', '+/')));\n        if (!$payload || !isset($payload->exp) || $payload->exp < time()) {\n            return null;\n        }\n        return $payload;\n    } catch (\\Exception $e) {\n        return null;\n    }\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - forged a JWT with a completely bogus/wrong secret (not the leaked jwt_secret) for user_id=1 and it was accepted by /api/profile, returning user 1's full profile data with HTTP 200. Signature is not verified at all server-side.

## 42. No balance check on external transfers — unlimited negative-balance transfer

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A04
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Services/TransferService.php:214-221 (referenced) and around line 280 (transferExternal DB write)
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
In `TransferService::transferExternal()`, the `if (in_array(...) && bccomp(...) < 0)` balance-check branch that exists in `transferOwn()` is absent. Account `updateBalance` is called with `-` for the debit amount with no precondition. An attacker (especially with the IDOR-source-account finding) can transfer huge sums from any account, pushing the balance arbitrarily negative. Financial integrity violation; in conjunction with the other transfer-service bugs this enables fraud.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed directly - transferred $50,000 from account 2 (balance $18,902.00) via external transfer, resulting new_from_balance was -$31,097.00, HTTP 201 success with no insufficient-funds rejection.

### Evidence
```
// In TransferService::transferOwn around line 119:\nif (in_array($fromAccount['account_type'], ['transaction', 'fx']) && bccomp($fromAccount['balance'], $debitAmount, 2) < 0) {\n    Response::error('INSUFFICIENT_FUNDS', 'Insufficient funds in the source account.', 400);\n}\n...\n// But in transferExternal — no equivalent check anywhere before:\nAccount::updateBalance($fromAccountId, '-' . $debitAmount);\n// direct debit happens with no balance precondition
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed directly - transferred $50,000 from account 2 (balance $18,902.00) via external transfer, resulting new_from_balance was -$31,097.00, HTTP 201 success with no insufficient-funds rejection.

## 43. Sensitive data exposure — every user-bearing endpoint returns password_hash and totp_secret in plaintext

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A02
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Models/User.php:60-77
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
`User::toPublic()` includes the raw `password_hash` and `totp_secret` fields in the user payload. This function is used by AuthController::register, AuthController::login, ProfileController::show, and ProfileController::update. As a result: registration response, login response, profile GET, and profile PUT all leak the credentials + 2FA secret of the authenticated user. Combined with the MD5 password hashing, those hashes are instantly crackable. Affects any request to /api/auth/register, /api/auth/login, /api/profile.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - registration response includes password_hash field in plaintext (MD5 digest) for the newly created user.

### Evidence
```
public static function toPublic(array $user): array\n{\n    return [\n        ...\n        'totp_enabled'  => (bool)$user['totp_enabled'],\n        'password_hash' => $user['password_hash'],\n        'totp_secret'   => $user['totp_secret'],\n    ];\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - registration response includes password_hash field in plaintext (MD5 digest) for the newly created user.

## 44. SQL injection in admin customer search via unsanitized `search` query parameter

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A03
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Controllers/AdminUserController.php:23-44
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
GET /api/admin/customers concatenates $_GET['search'] directly into the WHERE clause of two raw `query()` calls (count + fetch). No parameterisation, no escaping, no whitelist. Any authenticated admin (or attacker who has obtained an admin JWT) can inject arbitrary SQL — UNION SELECT to dump admin password hashes, error-based extraction, or stacked DDL via PDO (PDO::ATTR_EMULATE_PREPARES is false, so multi-statement is normally blocked, but UNION/sub-select still works for data exfiltration).

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - authenticated as admin via default creds admin/admin123, GET /api/admin/customers?search=test' triggered MySQL SQLSTATE[42000] syntax error disclosing raw injected SQL in WHERE clause.

### Evidence
```
$search = $_GET['search'] ?? '';\n...\nif ($search !== '') {\n    $where = \"WHERE first_name LIKE '%{$search}%' OR last_name LIKE '%{$search}%' OR email LIKE '%{$search}%'\";\n}\n// Count\n$countSql = \"SELECT COUNT(*) FROM users {$where}\";\n$stmt = $db->query($countSql);\n...\n$sql = \"SELECT id, email, first_name, last_name, phone, totp_enabled, created_at FROM users {$where} ORDER BY id DESC LIMIT {$perPage} OFFSET {$offset}\";\n$stmt = $db->query($sql);
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - authenticated as admin via default creds admin/admin123, GET /api/admin/customers?search=test' triggered MySQL SQLSTATE[42000] syntax error disclosing raw injected SQL in WHERE clause.

## 45. SQL injection in customer transaction listing via unsanitized `sort` query parameter (ORDER BY clause)

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A03
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Models/Transaction.php:34-58
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
GET /api/transactions passes the user-controlled `$_GET['sort']` value straight into the ORDER BY clause via double-quoted string interpolation in `Transaction::findByUser()`. Since ORDER BY position is not bindable as a parameter in MySQL, and there is no whitelist/validation, the attacker can inject SQL fragments. Classic exploit: error-based data exfiltration using subqueries into ORDER BY expression, or boolean/time-based blind.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed via error-based SQLi - sort=created_at;SELECT SLEEP(3) and sort=(SELECT SLEEP(3)) both triggered MySQL SQLSTATE[42000] syntax errors disclosing the raw injected SQL in the ORDER BY clause, proving unsanitized interpolation.

### Evidence
```
$sort = $_GET['sort'] ?? 'created_at';\n...\n$result = Transaction::findByUser($userId, $page, $perPage, $accountId, $sort);\n// in Transaction.php:\n$stmt = $db->prepare(\n    \"SELECT t.* FROM transactions t\n     WHERE (t.from_account_id = ? OR t.to_account_id = ?)\n     ORDER BY t.{$sort} DESC\n     LIMIT ? OFFSET ?\"\n);\n...\n$stmt = $db->prepare(\n    \"SELECT t.* FROM transactions t\n     JOIN accounts a ON t.from_account_id = a.id\n     WHERE a.user_id = ?\n     ORDER BY t.{$sort} DESC\n     LIMIT ? OFFSET ?\"\n);
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed via error-based SQLi - sort=created_at;SELECT SLEEP(3) and sort=(SELECT SLEEP(3)) both triggered MySQL SQLSTATE[42000] syntax errors disclosing the raw injected SQL in the ORDER BY clause, proving unsanitized interpolation.

## 46. SSRF via avatar proxy — server-side `file_get_contents` on user-supplied URL with no scheme/host validation

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A10
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Controllers/ProfileController.php:85-119
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
POST /api/profile/avatar accepts a JSON body with a `url` field and passes it directly to `file_get_contents($data['url'])`. There is no scheme restriction (so `file://`, `gopher://`, `dict://`, etc. all work), no allow-list of hosts/IP ranges, no DNS pinning, and no anti-rebinding. The fetched content is base64-encoded and returned to the attacker. Because the URL is ALSO persisted into the `users.avatar_url` column, the fetch re-occurs on every profile load — turning this into a blind/reflected SSRF that can hit cloud metadata services (AWS 169.254.169.254, GCP metadata.google.internal) or read local files via `file:///etc/passwd`.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed directly - POST /api/profile/avatar with url=file:///etc/passwd returned base64-encoded content that decodes to the full /etc/passwd file. AWS metadata probe (169.254.169.254) returned FETCH_FAILED (no route in this environment), but file:// scheme SSRF/LFI is fully confirmed.

### Evidence
```
if (empty($data['url'])) {\n    Response::error('VALIDATION_ERROR', 'Avatar URL is required.', 422);\n}\n$context = stream_context_create([\n    'http' => [\n        'timeout' => 5,\n        'follow_location' => true,\n    ],\n]);\n$content = @file_get_contents($data['url'], false, $context);\n...\nUser::update((int)$auth['user']['id'], ['avatar_url' => $data['url']]);\n$encoded = base64_encode($content);
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed directly - POST /api/profile/avatar with url=file:///etc/passwd returned base64-encoded content that decodes to the full /etc/passwd file. AWS metadata probe (169.254.169.254) returned FETCH_FAILED (no route in this environment), but file:// scheme SSRF/LFI is fully confirmed.

## 47. Stored XSS via transaction description — rendered as raw HTML in customer dashboard and account detail views

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A03
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: public/banking/js/pages/dashboard.js:91 + public/banking/js/pages/accounts.js:140
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
Transfer `description` strings are persisted verbatim in the `transactions` table by the server (backend only enforces `string|max:255`; no HTML sanitisation). They are then injected into the DOM via `container.innerHTML = html;` in both `dashboard.js` (`renderTransactions`, line 91) and `accounts.js` (`renderDetailTransactions`, line 140) without calling `U.escapeHtml(...)` — the surrounding fields like `original_currency` DO call `U.escapeHtml` which makes the omission for `description` clearly intentional/vulnerable. An authenticated user can send a transfer with `description: '<img src=x onerror=alert(document.cookie)>'`, and any subsequent dashboard/account-detail load by any user (including via auto-refresh) executes the payload in their session origin.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - server stores transaction description verbatim (tested with &lt;img src=x onerror=...&gt; payload via POST /api/transfers/external, HTTP 201 with unsanitized description echoed back). Source code of dashboard.js confirmed renderTransactions() concatenates tx.description raw into innerHTML without escapeHtml, unlike other fields in same function.

### Evidence
```
// dashboard.js line 91 (description interpolated raw):\n'<p class=\"font-medium text-slate-900 truncate\">' + (tx.description || tx.type) + '</p>' +\n// accounts.js line 140 (same pattern):\n'<td class=\"px-6 py-3.5 text-slate-900 font-medium\">' + (tx.description || '—') + '</td>' +\n// Backend only validates max length, never sanitises (TransactionController.php:58, :88):\n'description'     => 'string|max:255',
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - server stores transaction description verbatim (tested with &lt;img src=x onerror=...&gt; payload via POST /api/transfers/external, HTTP 201 with unsanitized description echoed back). Source code of dashboard.js confirmed renderTransactions() concatenates tx.description raw into innerHTML without escapeHtml, unlike other fields in same function.

## 48. TOTP 2FA bypass on external transfers — transfer succeeds even when 2FA required and TOTP is either unconfigured or omitted

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A04
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Services/TransferService.php:242-254
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
When `checkTotpRequired()` returns required=true and the user supplies no `totp_code`, the code does NOT refuse the transfer. It either (a) silently proceeds if 2FA is not configured for the user, or (b) silently proceeds if 2FA is configured but the code field is empty — because the second branch only runs `TotpService::verify(...)` if `!empty($totpCode)`. Net result: every external transfer can be made without 2FA verification, defeating one of the core security controls.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed directly in the same request - transfer completed with totp_verified:false and no totp_code was supplied in the request body, proving TOTP is not enforced.

### Evidence
```
if ($totpCheck['required']) {\n    if (!$user['totp_enabled']) {\n        // Allow transfer to proceed without TOTP if user hasn't configured it\n        $totpVerified = false;\n    } elseif (!empty($totpCode)) {\n        if (!TotpService::verify($user['totp_secret'], $totpCode, $user['email'])) {\n            Response::forbidden('TOTP_INVALID', 'Invalid TOTP code. Please try again.');\n        }\n        $totpVerified = true;\n    }\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed directly in the same request - transfer completed with totp_verified:false and no totp_code was supplied in the request body, proving TOTP is not enforced.

## 49. Unauthenticated full database export — `GET /api/admin/export/users` returns every user (incl. password_hash), every account and every transaction

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A01
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/AdminRouter.php:42 (route definition) + src/Controllers/AdminUserController.php:128-141 (handler)
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
The route `GET /api/admin/export/users` is registered with `'auth' => false` (no JWT, no admin check required). The handler runs three unfiltered SELECTs on users, accounts and transactions, returning the entire database — including MD5 password hashes and TOTP secrets for every customer — to anyone who hits the URL. This is the worst kind of data-exfiltration misconfiguration: zero auth.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed via GET /api/admin/export/users with no auth header. Returned HTTP 200 with full users table dump including password hashes and totp secrets.

### Evidence
```
// In AdminRouter.php:\n$r->addRoute('GET', '/api/admin/export/users', ['handler' => [AdminUserController::class, 'exportAll'], 'auth' => false]);\n\n// In AdminUserController.php:\npublic static function exportAll(): void\n{\n    $db = AdminDatabase::getInstance();\n    $stmt = $db->query('SELECT * FROM users');\n    $users = $stmt->fetchAll();\n    $stmt = $db->query('SELECT * FROM accounts');\n    $accounts = $stmt->fetchAll();\n    $stmt = $db->query('SELECT * FROM transactions ORDER BY created_at DESC');\n    $transactions = $stmt->fetchAll();\n    Response::success([\n        'users'        => $users,\n        'accounts'     => $accounts,\n        'transactions' => $transactions,\n    ]);\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed via GET /api/admin/export/users with no auth header. Returned HTTP 200 with full users table dump including password hashes and totp secrets.

## 50. Unauthenticated secret/credential disclosure — `GET /api/health` leaks JWT secret and DB credentials

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A05
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Router.php:18-32
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
The `/api/health` route is registered without auth and the handler echoes back `jwt_secret`, `db_host`, `db_name`, `db_user` from `config/app.php`. An unauthenticated attacker can obtain the JWT secret and use it to mint admin tokens (admin JWT secret is configured separately, but the customer JWT secret is leaked — forging customer tokens becomes trivial). Additionally the exposed creds may be useful for pivot attacks if the app is multi-tenant.

'/../config/app.php';\n    Response::success([\n        'status'      => 'ok',\n        'php_version' => PHP_VERSION,\n        'server'      => $_SERVER['SERVER_SOFTWARE'] ?? 'unknown',\n        'db_host'     => $config['db_host'],\n        'db_name'     => $config['db_name'],\n        'db_user'     => $config['db_user'],\n        'jwt_secret'  => $config['jwt_secret'],\n        'environment' => getenv('APP_ENV') ?: 'production',\n    ]);\n}

## 52. Weak password hashing (MD5) for customer credentials

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A02
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Services/AuthService.php:24-29
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
`AuthService::hashPassword()` uses `md5($password)`. MD5 is unsalted and trivially reversible via rainbow tables. Combined with the unauthenticated `/api/admin/export/users` endpoint that dumps hashes, or the registered/logged-in user endpoint that leaks `password_hash` via `User::toPublic()`, every user password can be cracked in seconds. Affects every customer who registers (which permits 1-character passwords; see separate finding).

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - new registrations hash with unsalted MD5 (32-hex char digest observed: 694b9d51df47149ccd1aa8e3c0e10e60 for password "a"). Legacy accounts use bcrypt ($2y$10$) but new ones use MD5, consistent with source lead.

### Evidence
```
public static function hashPassword(string $password): string\n{\n    return md5($password);\n}\npublic static function verifyPassword(string $password, string $hash): bool\n{\n    // Support both legacy md5 and bcrypt hashes\n    if (strlen($hash) === 32) {\n        return md5($password) === $hash;\n    }\n    return password_verify($password, $hash);\n}
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - new registrations hash with unsalted MD5 (32-hex char digest observed: 694b9d51df47149ccd1aa8e3c0e10e60 for password "a"). Legacy accounts use bcrypt ($2y$10$) but new ones use MD5, consistent with source lead.

## 53. Weak password policy — single-character passwords accepted at registration

- Severity: info
- OWASP: A07
- Source: sast lead
- Validation: confirmed
- Affected URL: src/Controllers/AuthController.php:11-26
- CVSS: 0 (CVSS:3.1/AV:N/AC:L/PR:N/UI:N/S:U/C:L/I:L/A:N)

### Description
POST /api/auth/register validates `password => 'required|min:1|max:128'` — one character is enough. Combined with MD5 hashing the impact is huge: brute-forcing a 1-character MD5 space is trivial, and dictionary attacks succeed almost always.

### Impact
—

### Likelihood
—

### Recommendation
Confirmed - registered new account with password="a" (1 char), server returned HTTP 201 success.

### Evidence
```
$errors = Validator::make($data, [\n    'email'      => 'required|email|max:255',\n    'password'   => 'required|min:1|max:128',\n    'first_name' => 'required|string|max:100',\n    'last_name'  => 'required|string|max:100',\n]);
```

### Validation Note
Confirmed - registered new account with password="a" (1 char), server returned HTTP 201 success.
